<div class='navbar section' id='navbar'><div class='widget Navbar' id='Navbar1'><script type="text/javascript">
    function setAttributeOnload(object, attribute, val) {
      if(window.addEventListener) {
        window.addEventListener('load',
          function(){ object[attribute] = val; }, false);
      } else {
        window.attachEvent('onload', function(){ object[attribute] = val; });
      }
    }

          <div class="date-outer">
        
<h2 class='date-header'><span>Friday, January 27, 2012</span></h2>

          <div class="date-posts">
        
<div class='post-outer'>
<div class='post hentry'>
<a name='6800920865002790398'></a>
<h3 class='post-title entry-title'>
<a href='http://eloquentwoman.blogspot.com/2012/01/famous-speech-friday-margaret-edsons.html'>Famous Speech Friday: Margaret Edson's 2008 Smith College commencement address</a>
</h3>
<div class='post-header'>
<div class='post-header-line-1'></div>
</div>
<div class='post-body entry-content'>
<div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;">
<a href="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-OVEPah1j_Ts/TxscWTMS8jI/AAAAAAAADCA/kB-QrWmaRiw/s1600/2008_edson.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: right; float: right; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-left: 1em;"></a><a href="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-OVEPah1j_Ts/TxscWTMS8jI/AAAAAAAADCA/kB-QrWmaRiw/s1600/2008_edson.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" height="173" src="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-OVEPah1j_Ts/TxscWTMS8jI/AAAAAAAADCA/kB-QrWmaRiw/s320/2008_edson.jpg" width="320" /></a></div>
<span style="font-family: inherit;">Folks who work at universities have heard more commencement speeches than the rest of us--that's part of the job. So when reader Kathy Schuetz, a communicator at the University of Maryland, wrote to suggest </span><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Margaret_Edson" style="font-family: inherit;">Margaret Edson</a><span style="font-family: inherit;">'s 2008 commencement speech at Smith College and called it "remarkable," I took notice.</span><br />
<div>
<span style="-webkit-border-horizontal-spacing: 0px; -webkit-border-vertical-spacing: 0px; border-collapse: separate;"><span style="font-family: inherit;"><br /></span></span></div>
<div>
<span style="-webkit-border-horizontal-spacing: 0px; -webkit-border-vertical-spacing: 0px; border-collapse: separate;"><span style="font-family: inherit;">Edson, a public school teacher in Atlanta, also is the Pulitzer-Prize winning author of the play <i>Wit, </i>about a scholar of English literature who is in the hospital and dying of ovarian cancer. (There's <a href="http://www.broadway.com/shows/wit/">a current production</a> of the play on Broadway at this writing.) Edson, herself a Smith graduate, uses the occasion to speak about what happens in classroom teaching. She uses anaphora, the rhetorical device that repeats a word or series of words at the start of a series of sentences--in this case, to tell us that classroom teaching is all about nothing, for both teachers and students:</span></span><br />
<span style="-webkit-border-horizontal-spacing: 0px; -webkit-border-vertical-spacing: 0px; border-collapse: separate; font-family: inherit;"></span><br />
<blockquote class="tr_bq">
<span style="-webkit-border-horizontal-spacing: 0px; -webkit-border-vertical-spacing: 0px; border-collapse: separate; font-family: inherit;"><b>We bring nothing</b> into the classroom -- perhaps a text or a specimen. We carry ourselves, and whatever we have to offer you is stored within our bodies. <b>You bring nothing</b> into the classroom -- some gum, maybe a piece of paper and a pencil: nothing but yourselves, your breath, your bodies.&nbsp;<b>Classroom teaching produces nothing</b>. At the end of a class, we all get up and walk out. It&#8217;s as if we were never there....<b>Classroom teaching expects nothing.</b> There is no pecuniary relationship between teachers and students....<b>Classroom teaching withholds nothing</b>. I say to my young students every year, &#8220;I know how to add two numbers, but I&#8217;m not going to tell you.&#8221; And they laugh and shout, &#8220;No!&#8221; That&#8217;s so absurd, so unthinkable. What do I have that I would not give to you?</span></blockquote>
</div>
<div class="TemplaceCDEBodyCopy" style="background-color: white; outline-color: initial; outline-style: none; outline-width: initial;">
This is a speech by a lover of language, but even its eloquence is not the most remarkable thing about it: Edson never looks at her notes during this commencement address. That's because there are no notes. The speech, all 18-plus minutes of it, is entirely delivered without a script or notes of any kind. Here's what you can learn from this famous speech, which engages the audience in three ways:<br />
<ul>
<li><b>Set up a clever contrast to make your point: </b>Edson spends several paragraphs telling us about the "nothing" of classroom teaching, underscoring that it yields nothing tangible.&nbsp;<span style="background-color: white; font-family: inherit;">Along the way, you realize that this "nothing" is yet substantial, meaningful, rewarding. Then Edson&nbsp;</span><span style="background-color: white; font-family: inherit;">carries that line of thinking through and transitions it to the future that lies ahead for the graduates: "</span><span style="background-color: transparent; font-family: inherit;">If you can point to something, you might lose it, or you might break it, or someone might take it from you. As long as you store it inside yourself, it&#8217;s not going anywhere -- or it&#8217;s going everywhere with you." Suddenly, that nothing sounds pretty good.</span></li>
<li><span style="background-color: transparent; font-family: inherit;"><b>Sly humor suggests they're all in on the joke together:</b> Edson builds a bond with her audience from the start, using the typical salutations to honored guests and her hosts humorously (s</span><span style="background-color: transparent;">peaking of her</span><span style="background-color: transparent; font-family: inherit;"> own class, she deadpans, "<span style="background-color: white;">when the history of the college is written, the record will note that this class was the best looking"). She pokes fun at trite honorifics, gently, and uses an exaggerated tone to suggest she, too, finds some of the pomp a bit much. For college graduates and faculty who've heard one too many commencement addresses, it's just the right touch.</span></span></li>
<li><span style="background-color: transparent; font-family: inherit;"><span style="background-color: white;"><b>Speaking without notes: </b>&nbsp;<a href="http://eloquentwoman.blogspot.com/2010/01/speech-question-use-notes-or-not.html">Whether you use notes or not is up to you and the speaking situation--there's nothing wrong with it.</a>&nbsp;But here is a lovely example of what speaking without notes can do for a speech, particularly a commencement address with a big crowd to engage.&nbsp;</span></span><span style="background-color: white; font-family: inherit;">The lack of notes lets her address the audience while looking at it directly, something every audience craves. As a result, she connects with this audience in a way many commencement speakers never do.&nbsp;</span></li>
</ul>
</div>
<a href="http://www.smith.edu/events/commencement_speech2008.php">Read the transcript of the speech here</a>&nbsp;[link corrected], and do watch the video below, listening for the words she enphasizes vocally. What do you think of this famous speech? <i>(Photo from Smith College)</i><br />
<i><br /></i>
<iframe allowfullscreen="" frameborder="0" height="300" mozallowfullscreen="" src="http://player.vimeo.com/video/1085942?title=0&amp;byline=0&amp;portrait=0" webkitallowfullscreen="" width="400"></iframe><br />
<a href="http://vimeo.com/1085942">2008 Smith College Commencement Margaret Edson</a> from <a href="http://vimeo.com/smithcollege">Smith College</a> on <a href="http://vimeo.com/">Vimeo</a>.<br />
<i><br /></i>
<i>Looking for famous speeches by women? Check out <a href="http://eloquentwoman.blogspot.com/p/eloquent-woman-index-of-famous-womens.html">The Eloquent Woman Index of Famous Women's Speeches</a>, with a wide variety of women speakers, types of speeches and topics to inspire your next speech. Each one comes with lessons for speakers, plus video or audio and a transcript, where available.</i>
<div style='clear: both;'></div>
</div>
<div class='post-footer'>
<div class='post-footer-line post-footer-line-1'><span class='post-author vcard'>

      }

      #uds-searchControl .gs-result .gs-title a:visited,
      #uds-searchControl .gs-result .gs-title a:visited * {
        color:#888888;
      }

      #uds-searchControl .gs-relativePublishedDate,
      #uds-searchControl .gs-publishedDate {
        color: #777777;
      }

      #uds-searchControl .gs-result a.gs-visibleUrl,
      #uds-searchControl .gs-result .gs-visibleUrl {
        color: #2288bb;
      }

      #uds-searchControl .gsc-results {
        border-color: #eeeeee;
        background-color: #ffffff;
      }

      #uds-searchControl .gsc-tabhActive {
        border-color: #eeeeee;
        border-top-color: #eeeeee;
        background-color: #ffffff;
        color: #222222;
      }

      #uds-searchControl .gsc-tabhInactive {
        border-color: #eeeeee;
        background-color: transparent;
        color: #2288bb;
      }

      #uds-searchClearResults {
        border-color: #eeeeee;
      }

      #uds-searchClearResults:hover {
        border-color: #eeeeee;
      }

      #uds-searchControl .gsc-cursor-page {
        color: #2288bb;
      }

      #uds-searchControl .gsc-cursor-current-page {
        color: #222222;
      }
    </style>
